Fail-open is unchanged (a commit failure still never blocks the write or throws), but it is no longer silent: `appendMessage` now reports a failed commit through an injectable `CommitFailureReporter` (`reportCommitFailureToStderr` by default), so an untracked record surfaces instead of hiding until someone happens to `git status` `backlog/topics/`. `node repair-bl-topic-records.js <project-root>` (`extension/src/tools/repair-bl-topic-records.ts`) fixes the other half: for every done ticket whose record is missing its opener, it regenerates the summary through the same exported `messageTextForEvent` the live path uses (never a second formatter), inserts it before the existing completion, and commits the result — idempotent by content, so re-running it a second time changes nothing, and a record with no matching done ticket or an opener already present is left untouched. A repaired record gains an opener and a completion, not a full transcript — the messages in between were never captured and stay lost.

**A missed completion is reconciled from current state, not lost forever (BL-330).** `diffTaskCompleted` (`swarmEventStream.ts`) only ever emits a transition the bot was alive to witness — comparing the previous snapshot's done-set against the current one — so a ticket that flips to done while the bot is down, crash-looping, or running a stale build (BL-328's own proven failure mode: a 25-hour-stale build) has its completion missed permanently, with no catch-up pass. For close-only that just leaves a stray open topic; for the archive-then-delete path this epic is building toward it is a data-loss trap, since a missed completion means a topic that was never archived, and a later retention sweep could delete it and destroy an un-archived transcript. `topicReconciliation.ts`'s `reconcileTopicLifecycle` is the safety net beneath the diff path, never a replacement for it: for every DONE ticket with a mapped topic, it checks whether that ticket's completion summary is already in BL-329's own durable record (`isAlreadyReconciled`) and, if not, brings the topic to its completed state by calling `routeEvent`'s existing `TaskCompleted` path directly — reusing `routeCompletionEvent`'s post-then-close behavior rather than building a second notify mechanism. A ticket with no topic ever mapped is skipped (reconciliation never creates a topic just to close it), and an in-flight ticket's topic is never touched. Idempotency is structural: an active/paused ticket is never even offered to this function (callers pass only `folders.done`), and an already-reconciled done ticket is detected via the same durable record BL-329 already maintains, not a second marker file. Wired into `telegram-front-desk-bot.ts`'s tick loop right after the existing Concierge tick, so it runs on every tick as a genuine safety net, not a separate manually-invoked sweep.

**Running headless (BL-292).** All six slices above were built, but nothing actually ran them outside a manually-started VS Code host — `startBridge` (`bridgeServer.ts`) was only invoked by the `swarmforge.startBridge` command, and the Front Desk Bot poller had no launcher at all, so the human's exported Telegram credentials reached nothing. A supervised headless launcher (`swarmforge/scripts/launch_front_desk.sh` + `front_desk_supervisor.bb`, mirroring `handoffd_supervisor.bb`'s bounded-restart-with-backoff and `launch_operator.sh`'s idempotent pid-guard) now brings up both: a thin CLI entrypoint (`extension/src/tools/start-bridge-headless.ts`) starts the bridge headless on a fixed port with a provisioned control token, and the Front Desk Bot is launched against it with the same token — the bridge comes up first, since the bot authenticates against it. No tunnel is needed for the front desk itself: the bot polls Telegram outbound and talks to the bridge over localhost; the front desk is now fully headless-capable. (Making the holistic UI itself reachable from a phone still needs an external tunnel — a separate, not-yet-built follow-on; this launcher serves the holistic UI headless only as a byproduct.)

**Reply egress is at-least-once, not at-most-once (BL-320).** The SSE reply relay in the diagram above (`bridgeServer.ts`'s `relayNewReplyOutboxEntries`) previously advanced its cursor the moment an outbox entry was *written* to the SSE socket, not when the bot actually received it — a dropped connection mid-relay (the real, observed undici `"terminated"` failure, same class as BL-302's inbound-poll crash) silently ate the reply, and the cursor lived only in memory, so a bridge restart forgot everything already relayed. This is the human's primary channel to the swarm when everything else is parked, so a silently dropped reply looked exactly like "the swarm ignored me" — the failure the whole front-desk epic (BL-274) exists to eliminate. The cursor is now ack-driven and persisted (`replyRelayCursor.ts`, `.swarmforge/operator/telegram-reply-relay-cursor.json`): `operator_reply.bb` stamps each outbox line with a UUID id, the bridge exposes `POST /reply-ack`, and `ackedIndex` only advances when the ack matches the entry actually sitting at the cursor — a stale or out-of-order ack leaves it unchanged rather than corrupting it forward. Every `/events` connection (fresh or reconnected) replays every still-unacked entry against a fresh read of the persisted cursor, so a bridge restart resumes from genuinely-delivered position; the bot tracks `seenId`s across its own reconnects so a redelivered-but-already-sent entry is re-acked, never re-posted to Telegram. `telegram-front-desk-bot.ts`'s `subscribeReplies` now wraps the SSE connection in its own retry-forever, capped-backoff reconnect loop (reusing BL-302's backoff helpers) so a dropped socket or failed ack triggers a real replay, not just the outer whole-process restart net.

**A restricted front-desk Operator answers the human even mid-attended-session (BL-334, fixes BL-333's alarm).** The single-Operator guard (`operator-running?`, keyed on one tmux session) was built to stop two Operators taking conflicting actions on the swarm — but an attended (interactive) Operator, told never to exit while the human is mid-conversation, permanently holds that one slot, so no disposable Operator was ever spawned to read the front desk: inbound Telegram messages queued unread for as long as the attended session ran (two days, once). The fix admits a SECOND Operator (`should-launch-front-desk-operator?`) that exists only to serve the front desk, eligible exactly when the full Operator is running (mutually exclusive with the full Operator's own launch decision by construction, both reading the same `full-operator-running?` fact within one tick) and gated by its own liveness guard (`front-desk-operator-running?`, its own tmux socket/pid file — never the full Operator's) so it never double-launches while one run is still in flight. It claims only `TELEGRAM_TOPIC_MESSAGE` events, into its own inflight file, never the events the full Operator has authority over, and both Operators' liveness is reported in one atomic `status.json` write (the front-desk Operator's state nested under `:front_desk`) so neither can overwrite the other's report. The restriction is structural, not a prompt instruction: the front-desk Operator launches headless as `claude -p --tools ""` — every tool removed from the session, no `--dangerously-skip-permissions`, no `--remote-control` — with all context it could need inlined into one self-contained one-shot prompt, and only its captured text completion (never a tool call it makes itself) reaches the trusted runtime, which alone performs the actual reply-outbox/thread-transcript write. This does not change attended mode's longevity — the human's interactive session is not cut short to serve the front desk — and BL-333's own front-desk-starvation alarm (an internal `status.json` signal, not user-facing) stays in place as the safety net for whatever this misses.

**A standing Operator topic — one always-there front door to the restricted Operator (BL-346).** The human's own request during a 2026-07-13 swarm-recovery incident: a persistent, pinned Telegram topic that IS the Operator's home, so he never has to hunt for the right `SUP-###` thread or wait for one to exist. `decideEnsureOperatorTopicAction` (`extension/src/tools/telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts`) is the reserved-subject twin of the existing BL-topic router's `decideTopicAction`: reuse the topic already bound to a stable, reserved `OPERATOR_SUBJECT_ID` constant, or `create` if no binding exists yet. `ensureOperatorTopic` (`telegram-front-desk-bot.ts`) calls the existing `createForumTopic` once if that binding is absent and writes it into the same `telegram-topic-map.json` the `SUP-###` routing already reads — called once in `main()`, **before** any of the bot's polling loops start, so no inbound message can ever reach the loop before the binding exists. This closes the auto-adopt trap the ticket exists to prevent: without a pre-bound reservation, the first message into a freshly-created "Operator" topic would silently be adopted as just another disposable `SUP-N` issue thread by the existing `open-for-topic` branch, exactly as happened to three earlier hand-made topics. A message in the reserved topic reaches the restricted, `--tools ""` front-desk Operator (BL-334's boundary, completely untouched — this ticket's diff touches zero `.bb` files) as a standing conversation, reusing BL-281's transcript + long-term-memory prompt seam unchanged; the reply lands back in the same topic via the existing reply-outbox → SSE → `resolveReplyTopicId` egress. A failed topic create degrades quietly (logged, never thrown) and never blocks ordinary `SUP-###`/`BL-###` routing; the next restart retries, since the map still lacks the binding. Out of scope, deliberately: detecting that a *recorded* topic id was deleted in Telegram (no send-failure detector for that exists yet — the same missing machinery BL-332's recreate-topic-from-record will need) and mirroring `operator.log` activity into the topic (the human confirmed conversation-only scope).

**An untagged gate now reaches the human instead of being dropped (BL-358).** `diffNeedsApproval` (`swarmEventStream.ts`) used to key a gate transition by the ticket the gated role currently holds and drop the gate entirely when the role held none — there was nowhere untagged to send it when that rule was written. Observed live: the specifier sat blocked on a question while draining an INTAKE file (holding no `BL-###` ticket), and that question reached the human nowhere — parked in a tmux pane until an operator happened to look, the exact BL-325 failure mode reappearing through the one seam BL-325 didn't cover. The standing Operator topic (BL-346, just above) now gives the untagged case a real destination, so the drop is no longer honest. `SwarmEvent.backlogId` becomes `string | null`: `null` identifies an untagged `NeedsApproval`, carrying the gated role instead (`swarmEventKey` dedups by role in that case, so a role that stays blocked across ticks is still asked only once). `topicRouter.ts`'s `routeUntaggedGateEvent` sends a null-`backlogId` event to the standing Operator topic (via a new `RouteAdapters.ensureOperatorTopic`, wired to the front-desk bot's existing `decideEnsureOperatorTopicAction`/`OPERATOR_SUBJECT_ID` binding) instead of the per-ticket reuse-or-create path, reusing `messageTextForEvent` unchanged rather than a second formatter. `conciergeTick.ts`'s retry-hold-back logic now reverts a failed untagged transition the same way it already reverts a tagged one, so a failed post to the Operator topic is retried on the next tick, not dropped for good. The answer path needed no new code: `handleApprovalDecisionForTicket`'s existing count-based fallback already answers correctly when a reply's thread (the Operator topic's reserved subject id) matches no role's held ticket — BL-325's loop, unmodified, now proven for the untagged case too. A role gated *while holding* a ticket is unaffected and still asks in that ticket's own topic.

**A reply now reaches the thread the human actually asked in, General included (BL-355).** Any message posted in a forum supergroup's General topic carries no `message_thread_id`, so `telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts` filed it under the single default support subject (`DEFAULT_SUBJECT_KEY`) and the reply relay routed the answer to that subject's mapped SUP topic — never back to General. The human hit this for real: five questions asked in General across one day, each answered, none visibly, so the swarm looked dead when it was not. `resolveReplyDelivery` (`telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts`) replaces the old bare-topic-id resolution with an explicit three-way outcome (`ReplyDelivery`): a subject with a real topic binding (SUP or, via the backlog map, `BL-###`) keeps going to that topic exactly as before, preserving its conversation history; a subject whose *only* binding is the default (General) now delivers the full reply with no `message_thread_id`, which Telegram routes straight back to General — previously this case resolved to `undefined` and `relayOneRecord`'s existing guard silently dropped it; and a subject bound to both a real topic *and* the default (the human's exact symptom: asked in General, answered only in a topic he never opens) delivers the full answer to its real topic but also posts a short, fixed pointer (`REPLY_POINTER_TEXT`) into General via `deliverReply`, so asking from General can never again look like silence even when the canonical answer lives elsewhere. `hasDefaultBinding` detects that dual-binding case by checking whether `DEFAULT_SUBJECT_KEY` itself resolves to the same subject. Subject filing and transcript storage are unchanged — this only changes where the reply is *delivered*.

**Role benchmarking, slice 1 — the coder role, several models, one real end-to-end report (BL-340).** The human's own hand-written framework spec: existing benchmarks (HumanEval, SWE-Bench, LiveCodeBench) measure models as generic programmers, but SwarmForge assigns *specialised* roles, each wanting a different model, and he wants an evidence base for "best", "best value", and "cheapest acceptable" per role. This slice proves the harness on the narrowest useful case — the coder role, because it is the most objectively measurable (tests pass / compile) — against 2-3 configured models, end to end, producing a real report; the other seven roles, every provider, and historical trends are later slices, and the leaderboard itself (BL-347) is documented in its own entry below. `extension/src/benchmark/runBenchmark.ts` runs every configured model sequentially (never fanned out — repeated real CLI calls against the same provider account stay bounded) against the same pinned task fixture (`taskFixture.ts#loadTaskSpec`, `extension/test/fixtures/benchmark/coder-task-01`), each model repeated `repetitions` times via `runTrial.ts` so run-to-run variance is measurable rather than hidden; `aggregate.ts` reduces each model's runs to mean/stddev quality, cost, duration and token usage. A provider that cannot execute shell actions autonomously (`providerCapability.ts` — today just `aider`, mirroring its existing simulate-not-act limitation elsewhere in the swarm) is excluded structurally before any trial runs, rather than being scored as though it completed the task. `rank.ts#rankModels` produces the three answers the spec asked for — highest mean quality, highest quality-per-dollar, and the cheapest model whose mean quality still clears an explicit, stated `qualityThreshold` — with `noAcceptableModelReason` naming why when nothing clears it, never a silently empty field. Production wires a real `claudeCliExecutor.ts` (actually invoking the Claude CLI) and a real `nodeTestQualityEvaluator.ts` (actually running the fixture's test suite) — the harness is built around one fakeable `ModelExecutor`/`QualityEvaluator` port pair, the same shape `recruiter/candidate.ts` already uses, so tests substitute fakes without a second harness. The report is not just produced but *committed*: `reportArtifact.ts#writeBenchmarkReport`/`commitBenchmarkReport` write `docs/benchmarks/<date>.json` and git-commit only that file, mirroring `costHealthSidecar.ts`'s own committed-sidecar pattern exactly — the one sanctioned way a live, machine-local benchmark run's numbers cross into git-derivable state, since BL-347's leaderboard can only ever read git-derivable state. `run-role-benchmark.ts` is the CLI entry point (`node run-role-benchmark.js <fixture-dir> <models-file> <repetitions> <quality-threshold> <target-repo-path>`), its `main()` a thin dispatcher over the exported, in-process-testable `parseArgs`, per this codebase's CLI convention. A real run against real models is committed at `docs/benchmarks/2026-07-13.json`.

**Role benchmarking, slice 2 — the Role Leaderboard reaches the human on the backlog dashboard (BL-347).** The human, asked which of three readings of "think of the presentation layer" he meant, picked benchmark results only — cost-per-ticket (BL-338) and burn rate (BL-273) stay deliberately unmerged — and, on the separate question of destination, chose the static backlog-dashboard PWA: reference data that changes only when a benchmark runs wants an always-available home, not a daily re-send that would become briefing-email noise. Because a benchmark run is inherently machine-local and live, its numbers can only cross into the PWA (a pure, git-SHA-reproducible projection with no bridge/host connectivity per `local-engineering.prompt`) the same sanctioned way `costHealth` already does: `computeBacklogDashboard` folds in the latest COMMITTED `docs/benchmarks/<date>.json` (BL-340) verbatim as a new optional `roleLeaderboard` field, mirroring `readLatestCostHealthSidecar` — null on missing/malformed, never thrown, absent (not empty) when nothing has ever been committed. The PWA renders it as its first-ever `<table>` (every other section is a list or inline SVG chart, so this adds table markup and CSS to `pwa/index.html`, and with it a service-worker shell-hash bump), a collapsible "Role Leaderboard" section (`renderAll`, `pwa/app.js`) naming BEST / BEST VALUE / CHEAPEST ACCEPTABLE per role alongside the stated quality threshold, the run date, and the reported variance, hidden entirely rather than rendered empty when no report has been committed — collapse state persists via the PWA's existing Cache Storage mechanism, never localStorage. `docs/reference/backlog-dashboard-schema.md` documents the new field in the same parcel, additive within `schemaVersion` 1.

**A quality tie is reported as a tie, never resolved into a false winner by array order (BL-385).** The committed benchmark run (`docs/benchmarks/2026-07-13.json`) has haiku, sonnet and opus all landing at `meanQuality: 1`, `qualityStdDev: 0` — a perfect three-way tie — but `rank.ts`'s original `maxBy` reduced with a strict `>`, so a tie silently kept whichever model happened to be first in the config array, and the leaderboard (BL-347) told the human "Best: Claude Haiku" on the strength of array ordering, not a measurement; `bestByValue` and `cheapestAcceptable` inherited the same false confidence, since with quality identical across the board both reduce to plain cheapest. `rank.ts` now derives a tie honestly: `topScorers` collects every candidate sharing the top score (never fewer or more, regardless of array order) and `bestByQualityOf` reports `{ modelId: null, tied: true }` whenever two or more candidates share it — a genuine single winner among otherwise-tied runners-up is still named (the tie path must never swallow a real winner). `BenchmarkRanking` gains two fields: `couldNotDiscriminateReason` (non-null exactly when `bestByQuality` is null because of a real tie, distinct from `noAcceptableModelReason`'s separate "no candidates at all" case) and `bestByValueRankedByCostAlone` (true when best-value was computed under a quality tie — cheapest is still a defensible answer, but it must be labelled a ranking on cost alone, never presented as a quality-cost judgement). The leaderboard (`pwa/app.js#renderRoleLeaderboard`) renders a full-width reason row in place of a "Best" row when tied, and relabels "Best value" to "Best value (ranked by cost alone — quality tied)" via a new locale string in `pwa/locales.js`, in both English and French. The reported result does not depend on model array order — the property a `>`-vs-`>=` mutant would most easily restore — verified directly against the real committed three-way-tied fixture, shuffled.

**The battery can actually separate models (BL-386).** BL-385 stopped the leaderboard *reporting* a false winner; this is what gives it something true to report. The harness previously ran exactly one hardcoded task — `coder-task-01-word-frequency`, one exported function in one file, no dependencies — which every model one-shot for a perfect `meanQuality: 1`, `qualityStdDev: 0` three-way tie: the task was too easy to discriminate on, not the metric too coarse (quality is already fractional, `testsPassed / testsTotal`). `RunBenchmarkParams.task` became `tasks: TaskSpec[]`; `runBenchmark.ts` now loops every task × every model, `TrialOutcome` carries its own `taskId`, and `ModelAggregate` gains a per-task `taskScores` breakdown alongside `meanQuality`/`qualityStdDev`, which now reflect a model's showing across the whole battery rather than one task. `BenchmarkReport.taskId` (a single string) became `taskIds: string[]`. The battery gains a second, deliberately harder fixture, `coder-task-02-inventory-reservation`: a solution spanning two files (`inventory.js` + `reservations.js`) that holds an invariant — stock must never go negative — which only the hidden test suite enforces, never stated in the prompt; verified discriminating for real against three reference solutions (correct: 5/5, a naive one skipping the bounds check: 2/5, the raw stub: 0/5). `taskSoundness.ts` refuses a task whose own reference solution (a `reference/` overlay on the materialized fixture, never shown to a model) fails its own tests, before scoring any real model against it — the mirror-image bug (flooring every score instead of saturating them) this ticket's own mechanism could otherwise introduce; a fixture with no `reference/` directory is left unvalidated (sound by default), so the original `coder-task-01` fixture is unaffected. A hardener bounce closed one remaining edge: `roleForReport` (`pwa/app.js`) crashed when a battery root held zero tasks at all (`taskIds` *and* `refusedTasks` both empty — `loadTaskBattery` has no minimum-count guard) — it now returns `null` in that case and `renderRoleLeaderboard` hides the section, the same "hidden entirely, never rendered empty" contract the section already had for a wholly-absent report. `docs/reference/backlog-dashboard-schema.md` documents the `taskIds` shape change.

**The starvation alarm now arms on delivery, not on attempt (BL-345).** BL-333's alarm previously persisted `:armed?` — and so suppressed every further alarm for the whole starvation episode — *before* the alarm email was even sent, discarding `send-configured-email!`'s own success/failure result; a transient send failure at the exact tick starvation crossed the threshold silently swallowed the alarm for days (the second occurrence of this defect class in this library, after BL-215). `operator_lib.bb` replaces the old attempt-based decision with three pure functions: `classify-delivery-result` (delivered / terminal-misconfig / transient-failure, folding a test fixture's forced result into terminal so it is never mistaken for a real failure), `starvation-alarm-should-attempt?` (gates each tick on armed?/starving?/backoff-due), and `next-starvation-alarm-state` (arms on delivered or terminal-misconfig, retries a transient failure with a bounded, backed-off attempt count, and arms *and* logs loudly once the cap is reached). `operator_runtime.bb`'s `starvation-alarm-sweep!` now actually consults the send's result through those functions, configurable via `OPERATOR_ALARM_MAX_ATTEMPTS` (default 5), `OPERATOR_ALARM_BACKOFF_BASE_MS` (default 60000) and `OPERATOR_ALARM_BACKOFF_MAX_MS` (default 1800000). On exhausting the retry cap the escalation channel *is* the broken email, so the loud log line and `status.json` are the only remaining signals — accepted rather than worked around with a second notifier. A misconfigured channel (no recipient, no API key) is warned about once and never retried, since retrying a misconfiguration can never succeed.

**A stuck role's escalation now emails the human on a headless box too (BL-349).** BL-336's dark-emitter audit found a real gap of the same shape as BL-215/BL-333/BL-345: when a role sits stuck/gated past grace+cooldown, the live `handoffd.bb`'s `:on-stuck-escalation!` adapter only wrote `.swarmforge/daemon/chase-escalations.json` — the code that actually emails the human, `NeedsHumanEmailNotifier`, exists only in the VS Code extension host, so on a headless box (how this swarm actually runs) a role could sit stuck indefinitely with the file written and nobody reading it. `stuck_escalation_email_lib.bb` (new) adds the missing email leg, reusing the same shared `daemon_alarm_lib.bb/send-configured-email!` sender the daemon-death and starvation alarms already use — no second email path — and reapplies BL-345's exact delivery-based arming rule, this time per-role (multiple roles can be independently stuck at once, each with its own retry/backoff state): a transient send failure never arms and is retried with bounded, backed-off attempts; a terminal misconfiguration warns once and arms; exhausting the retry cap arms and logs loudly rather than retrying forever. Wired into `handoffd.bb`'s existing `:on-stuck-escalation!` adapter alongside the unchanged `write-escalation!` call — the file record still exists exactly as before, the email is additive. Edge-triggered per-role: a role that stays stuck is not re-emailed on every sweep, and a role that recovers and later re-sticks starts from fresh (never-attempted) state and emails again. Out of scope, deliberately: retiring the extension-host `NeedsHumanEmailNotifier` (left as-is), and the other four findings from the same BL-336 audit (BL-350 through BL-353).

**The swarm now pushes its own work to origin, so it never looks dead from outside (BL-356).** Nothing in the swarm ever ran `git push`: publication depended entirely on an LLM role remembering to, and twice in one day it silently didn't — six commits sat local overnight until 04:00, then three more hours of closed/specced tickets sat local while `origin/main` stayed frozen, both times indistinguishable from a dead swarm until a human noticed and pushed by hand. `push_sweep_lib.bb` (new) is the pure decision/state logic for a periodic "does main need publishing?" sweep, wired into `handoffd.bb`'s existing sweep cadence the same way `recert-notify-sweep!`/`dead-letter-notify-sweep!` are — no new scheduling mechanism. Each tick compares local `main` against `origin/main`'s ahead/behind counts and takes one of three paths: **should-push** (local ahead, origin not ahead) pushes with a bounded, backed-off retry budget, raising a push-failure alarm only once that budget is exhausted; **diverged** (origin carries commits local doesn't) never force-pushes — it raises a divergence alarm and leaves reconciliation to a human; **nothing-to-push** clears all persisted state. Both alarms reuse `daemon_alarm_lib.bb/send-configured-email!` and reapply the BL-345/BL-349 delivery-based arming rule: a transient send failure never arms and retries bounded; a terminal misconfiguration warns once and arms. An architect bounce (2026-07-14) caught a cross-episode gap the first cut missed: the push-failure and divergence alarms each kept their own `armed?` flag, but only the two terminal transitions (`:nothing-to-push`, `:pushed`) cleared them — entering `:diverged` never cleared a stale `:should-push` alarm, and returning from `:diverged` to `:should-push` never cleared a stale divergence alarm, so a second, unrelated episode of either kind could be silently swallowed by a prior episode's leftover armed flag. Fixed by clearing the *other* transition's stale flag on entry to each branch, so every episode — not just the first — gets its own alarm.

**A closed ticket can still be invisible to the human — verify against his real surface, not a test (BL-335).** Three tickets (BL-260, BL-252/BL-290, BL-286) were done, merged and closed, yet the human reported each feature missing after the fix that was supposed to deliver it. Investigating each against the surface he actually looks at (the real briefing email, the real deployed PWA — never a local render or a passing test) found all three were **stale-build complaints**: the daemon process serving him had been running pre-fix compiled code, not that the fixes were broken or unwired. Two were email-side (the daemon's own `RESEND_API_KEY` was absent for its entire history until a `2026-07-11T11:13:43Z` restart, so several already-merged fixes hadn't reached a live send yet); the PWA half of the diagram request was neither stale nor broken — it was **never in scope** (BL-260 shipped the email side only) and is raised as a separate feature request rather than silently built or dropped here. While verifying, this surfaced a live, reproducible gap in BL-328's own build-freshness tool: `build_freshness_cli.bb`'s `run-sync!` only triggered `recompile-extension!` when the **`:front-desk`** group (bridge/bot) was stale, but `handoffd.bb` and `operator_runtime.bb` themselves shell out to compiled `extension/out/tools/*.js` CLIs (`render-briefing-diagrams.js`, `suite-duration-line.js`, `emit-cost-health-sidecar.js`, `operator-decide.js`, and others) — so a `:handoffd`- or `:operator`-only staleness left the shared `extension/out/` directory stale forever, since those groups' restarts (`restart-handoffd-group!`/`restart-operator-group!`) only respawn the Babashka process and never recompile. `node-stale?` now triggers a recompile on **any** staleness, not just `:front-desk` — cheap when unneeded, and the only way to never silently miss a case again.

**A swarm launched by a short-lived caller no longer dies when that caller exits (BL-372).** `start-swarm.sh:94` backgrounded `./swarm` without detaching it from its own session/process group, so a swarm launched from a disposable Operator window (or any other short-lived shell) came up correctly and then died minutes later when the caller exited — observed twice in one day as a fresh 8x `AGENT_EXITED` storm, indistinguishable from a crash. `handoffd.bb`'s own `nohup … &` idiom (`start_handoff_daemon.sh:68`) already survived the same class of exit; `start-swarm.sh` now backgrounds `./swarm` the same portable way and captures the launch job's pid. A required self-check asserts the launch actually detached and fails loudly, non-zero, naming the defect, rather than silently shipping a swarm that is still one caller-exit away from dying — the human's explicit design call was to fix the wrapper only (`start-swarm.sh`), not `swarmforge/scripts/swarmforge.sh` itself (a tracked upstream fork deviation, per the local-engineering Architecture Rules), accepting that a bare `./swarm` invocation bypassing the wrapper is still reapable. An architect review caught the first cut's self-check as vacuous: it compared the tmux **server's** ppid to the caller's pid, but tmux's server unconditionally self-daemonizes and reparents away from its caller within a fraction of a second regardless of whether the launch used `nohup` — so it reported "detached" against a real tmux server on every real invocation, including the pre-fix broken path (confirmed by direct reproduction, twice). The fix checks the **launch job's own** `SIGHUP`-ignored disposition (`nohup`'s direct, immediate effect, read from `/proc/<pid>/status`'s `SigIgn` line on Linux, with a best-effort `ps -o sigignore=` fallback for macOS/BSD) immediately after backgrounding it, since that state is no longer checkable once the launch job exits normally — a signal proven (against a real `tmux new-session`, both with and without `nohup`) to actually discriminate a working fix from a broken one. The readiness gate (`wait_for_ready`, unaffected by any of this) still polls socket/session state directly and still exits non-zero if the swarm never comes up, so detaching the server's lifetime never traded away the wrapper's synchronous "did it actually start" result.

**Losing control of the swarm is no longer misread as all 8 agents dying (BL-368).** Live incident, 2026-07-14 ~02:06: the tmux socket was unlinked (BL-367) while the tmux server, all 8 role processes, and `handoffd` were still alive and running — but the health sweep read `agents_running: 0` and enqueued eight false `AGENT_EXITED` events, whose scripted recovery is to *relaunch the role*. Acting on those would have started a second set of eight agents on the same worktrees as eight still-running ones — concurrent commits and racing merges on every branch at once, corrupting the repo; only a disposable Operator's on-the-day reasoning caught it, and that judgment call was never meant to be load-bearing. Two independent layers, since either alone leaves the failure reachable: (1) **don't misdiagnose** — `operator_runtime.bb`'s `tmux-control-status` now distinguishes "tmux answered, here is its (possibly empty) session list" from "a control channel that used to exist stopped responding" (a socket pointer file present but tmux itself erroring, exactly BL-367's incident shape); only the latter raises the new `SWARM_CONTROL_LOST` event, logged unconditionally to `runtime.log`, and `dead-agent-events` now only ever runs against a confirmed-reachable session list, never a guess — a swarm that never launched tmux (or a test fixture, no pointer file at all) stays the ordinary empty-sessions case, since you cannot lose control of something never established. (2) **don't act dangerously even if misdiagnosed** — `swarmforge.sh`'s `create_role_session` (and its two callers, the main launch loop and `role_lifecycle.sh`'s unpark) now refuses to relaunch a role whose own heartbeat file (`extension/src/tools/heartbeat.ts`, refreshed by the `claude` process itself on every tool call, entirely tmux-independent) names a still-live pid — holding regardless of what tmux reports, one shared guard closing every relaunch path (`./swarm`, `start-swarm.sh`, unpark). Proven against real tmux servers, real killed sessions, and a real live process backing a real heartbeat pid (never mocked), including the ticket's explicit "don't break the real path" requirement: a socket that IS genuinely reachable with one session genuinely gone still reports exactly that role exited, so the real detection still fires. 4/4 acceptance scenarios green (`specs/features/BL-368-control-loss-is-not-agent-death.feature`). Out of scope, deliberately: *where* the socket lives (BL-367) — this fix holds even if BL-367 relocates it, since socket loss is not the only way the control channel can go away.

**A done ticket's topic is only ever deleted after its content is verified AND durably git-committed (BL-331).** Telegram gives a forum topic exactly three verbs — close (locks, stays visible), reopen, delete (removes it, destroys history) — so the 21-and-growing list of closed-but-still-listed topics can only be shortened by deletion, and deletion is irreversible; this ticket is the thing that makes calling it safe, building on BL-329's per-ticket record and BL-330's reconciliation sweep (both hard prerequisites — deleting before either existed would have meant no fallback at all). `decideTopicDeletion`/`sweepTopicDeletions` (`extension/src/concierge/topicDeletion.ts`), wired into `telegram-front-desk-bot.ts`'s tick body right after reconciliation, walk every done ticket and delete its topic (`deleteForumTopic`, already present on `telegramClient.ts`) only when all of: a topic is actually mapped, the retention window has elapsed (a recently-done ticket's topic stays glanceable in the app for N days), and the ticket's completion summary is verified present in BL-329's own durable record — any other case (no record, retention window still open, no topic ever mapped) defaults to `keep`, and an unverified-but-eligible ticket surfaces loudly via an adapter-injected reporter rather than failing silently toward deletion. Deleting drops the `backlogId → topicId` mapping (`dropBacklogTopicMapping`) so nothing later posts into the dead thread id. **Architect bounce, same ticket:** the first cut's "verified" check (`hasCompletionRecord`) only proved the completion text *parses* from whatever is on disk right now — it never confirmed the record was actually git-committed, and `appendMessage`'s own write can succeed while its commit fails (the exact window `blTopicStore.ts`'s `CommitFailureReporter` exists for), so a record could read back "complete" while still an uncommitted working-tree file, lost on a fresh checkout/git clean/disk failure. `isFileCommitted` (`gitCommitScopedFile.ts`) checks `git status --porcelain` scoped to exactly one path (fails closed — any git error, including "not a repo", reads as *not* committed) and `isRecordCommitted` (`blTopicStore.ts`) wraps it for a ticket's record path; `decideTopicDeletion` now takes a `recordIsCommitted` boolean and treats a verified-but-uncommitted record as unverified, identical to a missing one. Proven against real git repos: an uncommitted write is never deleted, and the identical record, once actually committed, is. E2E QA proved the failure case against a real induced archive-write failure (never mocked), confirming deletion does not outrun its archive. 5/5 acceptance scenarios green (`specs/features/BL-331-delete-only-after-verified-serialisation.feature`). Out of scope, deliberately: recreating a topic by replaying its record (BL-332) — the epic is not complete, and deletion is not provably safe end-to-end, until that round trip is proven too.

**A deleted topic's history is not actually gone — it can be recreated from its own repo record (BL-332).** This closes the archive-then-delete epic: BL-331 made deletion safe to *call*, but safe-to-call is not the same as *reversible*, and this is the slice that proves the round trip. `decideTopicRestore` (`extension/src/concierge/topicRecreation.ts`) picks the cheap path whenever it can: if the ticket's topic id is still mapped, the topic was only ever closed, never deleted, so `reopenForumTopic` (already on `telegramClient.ts`) gets it back byte-identical with no rendering at all. Only once the mapping is genuinely gone (BL-331's `dropBacklogTopicMapping` already dropped it as part of the same delete) does `recreateTopicFromRecord` do the expensive thing: create a fresh topic, then replay BL-329's durable `backlog/topics/<ticketId>.json` record into it in original `seq` order. **Honest limit, stated up front to the human, not hidden:** Telegram will not let a bot repost history under its original authors or at its original timestamps, so a recreated topic is a labelled reconstruction (`reconstructionHeaderText`, posted first, before any replayed message, so it survives even an interrupted replay) — each replayed line instead renders its original author and timestamp *into the text itself* (`renderedMessageText`), the only way either survives at all. The read side is non-destructive by design: `readRecord` is a plain read, never a consume, so the same repo record can rebuild the same topic again and again. `recreate-bl-topic.js <project-root> <ticket-id>` (`extension/src/tools/recreate-bl-topic.ts`) is the one-shot CLI a human or the Operator runs to trigger either path — thin wiring only (real Telegram/filesystem adapters plus `runCliMain`), no decision logic of its own, mirroring `repair-bl-topic-records.ts`'s existing "thin CLI, real logic elsewhere" shape. A failed `createTopic` is a clean no-op: nothing is recorded and the record itself is untouched, so the human can simply retry. **QA bounce, same ticket:** the first cut awaited every `postMessage` during replay but discarded its boolean result, so a failed send during replay was indistinguishable from a successful one — `recordTopicId` still ran and the CLI still reported success, silently routing the ticket to a topic missing part of its history with no way to detect it. `recreateTopicFromRecord` now tracks every `postMessage` result across the header and each replayed message and only calls `recordTopicId` when the whole replay actually landed; a failure still attempts every remaining message rather than abandoning the reconstruction partway through, so a partial failure is never silently armed as if it were a full success. Out of scope, deliberately: detecting on its own that a topic was deleted (there is still no send-failure detector for that) — recreation today is human/Operator-triggered, not automatic.

**A Telegram message can no longer be silently destroyed on its way to the Operator (BL-369).** The incident: a human question reached the SUP-### thread transcript and then reached nobody — no Operator event, no wake, ~9h of silence — recovered only by a human restarting the bot and bridge. Four independent single-points-of-loss existed on the inbound path, and the first is what made every other one *permanent* rather than merely late: `pollAndForward` (`telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts`) computed `nextOffset` from the updates it *fetched*, with no regard to whether delivery actually succeeded — and acking an offset to Telegram is irrevocable, so an undelivered message was gone forever the moment the offset moved past it. `offsetAfterDelivery` replaces that: the offset now only ever advances past updates whose delivery was *confirmed*, stopping at the first undelivered one in fetch order so a later, successfully-delivered update in the same batch never gets acked ahead of an earlier failure. With that keystone fixed, the remaining three failure points degrade to retries instead of losses: (2) `postToBridge`'s `false` result is no longer discarded — it feeds directly into the new offset logic; (3) the bridge's ingest (`ingestTelegramInboundMessage`, `bridgeServer.ts`) replaces its old two-non-atomic-writes-no-error-path shape with a single function that dedupes by Telegram's own `update_id` first (a redelivered message with both its transcript line and its queued event already confirmed is a pure no-op), writes the transcript only if that `update_id` isn't already recorded, and marks `eventQueued` true *only after* the enqueue is confirmed to have not thrown — any exception anywhere in the sequence is caught and reported as `{success: false}` rather than left to hang the response or silently marked delivered; (4) the shared `.swarmforge/operator/events.jsonl` file — appended by the bridge (Node) and rewritten/deleted by four separate read-modify-write sites in `operator_runtime.bb` (Babashka) every tick, with no lock — now serializes all five sites (the bridge's append included) through a real filesystem mutex (`events-lock-dir`, an `mkdir`-as-lock, the same shape `swarm_handoff.bb`'s sequence counter already uses, bounded rather than spinning forever on a stuck lock) so a bridge append landing between another writer's read and its rewrite is no longer silently destroyed. A reconciliation sweep (`reconcile-unqueued-messages!`) is the safety net beneath all of that: it scans every thread transcript for a message that has an `update_id` but no confirmed `eventQueued`, and re-enqueues it — idempotent by construction, since the same `eventQueued` flag the bridge's own ingest sets is what makes the next sweep see nothing left to reclaim. Redelivery is now expected, not exceptional, so idempotency by `update_id` runs the full path (mirrors BL-320's existing id-based reply-outbox dedup posture rather than inventing a second one) — one transcript line and one wake for a message delivered twice, never two.

**The standing Operator conversation surface now survives a crash, an OOM kill, or a reboot with no human action (BL-359).** BL-346 built the durable Telegram topic and BL-334 the restricted disposable-run lane; this ticket is the missing supervision underneath both. Verified on the primary host before this ticket: `operator_runtime.bb` and `front_desk_supervisor.bb` were both orphaned `nohup` children of `start_operator_runtime.sh` with PPID 1 — nothing supervised either, so a crash or a WSL restart took the whole conversation surface down for good, exactly what happened during the 2026-07-13 ~9h outage. `generate_systemd_units.sh` (BL-304/BL-351, fixed by BL-366 so the rendered units can actually start — absolute `ExecStart`, `StartLimitIntervalSec` moved into `[Unit]`) already rendered both units correctly but had only one caller, `provision_secondary_host.sh`, a different host's from-scratch bootstrap that installs just the operator unit and never front-desk — "generated but never installed" is, in that script's own words, as dark as no unit at all. `swarmforge/deploy/provision_primary_host.sh` is the missing installer for *this* host: it reuses the same generator for both units (no duplicated unit-content logic), moves them into `/etc/systemd/system/`, pre-creates the per-pack secrets file at `/etc/swarmforge/<pack>.env` (root-owned, 600, matching the secondary-host precedent), then `daemon-reload`s and `enable --now`s both — idempotent and safely re-runnable, since `systemctl enable`/`enable --now` against an already-enabled unit is a no-op. `PROVISION_PRIMARY_DRYRUN=1` prints the install/enable commands instead of running them (no sudo, no real systemd mutation) while still generating the real unit files to a `/tmp` path, the seam its own test suite drives. The second half closes a standing lie in the code, not a missing feature: `operator-running?` (`operator_runtime.bb`) already checked exactly two signals — a tmux session on the Operator's own socket, or a live pid in `operator.pid` — and a comment already asserted "an attended (interactive) session holds the slot indefinitely by design," but the actual attended entry point was an untracked, hand-written `attend.sh` that was a bare `exec claude …` and registered neither signal. An attended human session was therefore invisible to the runtime: `should-launch-operator?` still read "not running" and spawned a second, unrestricted, tool-holding Operator beside the human, while BL-334's restricted front-desk lane stayed dark since its own gate requires `full-operator-running?`. `swarmforge/scripts/attend_operator.sh` is that design made true: it writes its own pid into `operator.pid` before launching `claude` in the foreground (the same signal the disposable launcher already registers, no new detection code needed), refuses to double-launch if a live pid is already registered, and removes the pid file on exit only if it still names this session — never clobbering a disposable run that legitimately claimed the slot afterward. This keeps the ticket's own standing constraint honest: an always-on Operator that silently disables the swarm's own health sweeps and stall recovery would be strictly worse than one that drops, because it would be invisible — under this design the presence never holds the slot indefinitely by stealth; it registers honestly or (for disposable runs) releases the slot on exit, so recovery keeps working underneath it. Out of scope, deliberately: rebuilding the standing topic, the reply routing, or the BL-334 second lane — all three already existed and are unmodified. This ticket's evidence is a live E2E procedure (kill the runtime and confirm unattended recovery, close the launching terminal, verify both units report `enabled`, confirm an attended session prevents a second unrestricted launch), not just a green unit suite — the human's own scope note on the ticket is explicit that code-complete is not proof here.

**The Operator can now pass a question down instead of sitting on it (BL-371).** Until this ticket the Operator's only two moves on a human question were answer it or silently sit on it — `operator_handoff.bb` requires a pre-existing `BL-###` ticket, so a raw question that isn't yet a ticket had no path at all, which is exactly what a human noticed and complained about during the 2026-07-13 outage. The Operator now always does exactly one of two things on a `TELEGRAM_TOPIC_MESSAGE` event: answer directly (`operator_reply.bb`, unchanged), or, when it judges it cannot answer correctly, file the question with the new `operator_file_question.bb` — which writes it as a raw intake item into the backlog **root**, the same channel the human's own raw asks already land in, so the specifier's existing drain-root-first order picks it up with zero new routing or queue machinery. Filing and telling the human happen in one call: `operator_file_question.bb` shells to the real `operator_reply.bb` itself to confirm what was filed, so the Operator never calls both separately. Which of the two applies is the Operator LLM's judgment call at reply time, not a mechanical classifier — the ticket builds the capability and the guarantee, not a decision procedure. The filed item is durably git-committed, not merely written: a commit failure is reported loudly (non-zero exit, never a false "filed" success) rather than the silent-loss failure mode BL-314 already taught this codebase, proven against a real independent clone (the only check that actually proves a different worktree can see it). Choosing which agent handles the filed question stays the coordinator's call, by the same constitutional rule that already keeps routing decisions out of the Operator's hands.

**The filed-intake Telegram confirmation is now a clickable GitHub permalink instead of a plain path (BL-415).** BL-371's `operator_file_question.bb` confirmed a filed question with plain text — `"Filed for the swarm: backlog/INTAKE-<slug>.md"` — a repo-relative path the human could read but not click. The reply is now composed by a new pure helper, `operator-lib/filed-intake-confirmation-text` (`operator_lib.bb`), which derives a commit-SHA GitHub blob permalink (`https://github.com/<owner>/<repo>/blob/<sha>/<rel-path>`) from the filing commit's own sha (already known at reply time — `write-and-commit-intake!` just made it) and the repo's `origin` remote, handling both the SSH (`git@github.com:owner/repo.git`) and HTTPS (`https://github.com/owner/repo.git`) remote forms via `github-base-from-remote-url`. A commit-SHA link is deliberate, not a `blob/main` shortcut: the specifier drains (git rm's) the intake file from the backlog root as soon as it's specced, which would 404 a branch-relative link within the hour, while the permalink keeps resolving to the file as filed. Telegram auto-links a bare `https://` URL in plain text, so no `parse-mode` change was needed — the reply keeps its human-readable rel-path too: `"Filed for the swarm: <rel-path> — <permalink>"`. Composing the confirmation can never block or fail the (already durably committed) filing: a missing or non-GitHub origin falls back to the original plain-path text rather than erroring. 3/3 acceptance scenarios green (`specs/features/BL-415-intake-reply-github-permalink.feature`).

**A live front-desk process is no longer mistaken for a listening one (BL-370).** Through the same ~9h inbound outage BL-369 fixes the data-loss half of, `.swarmforge/operator/front-desk-supervisor.status.json` reported `status: running` the whole way — the health signal tracked process liveness ("is there a pid"), not whether the poll loop was still consuming, so nothing self-healed because nothing knew anything was wrong. BL-345's starvation alarm cannot cover this: it fires on events pending and not draining, and in this failure mode no event ever arrives to become pending. The fix rests on one load-bearing point: a *quiet* front desk is not indistinguishable from a *dead* one, because a healthy bot long-polls Telegram with a bounded timeout (`POLL_TIMEOUT_SECONDS`) and so completes a poll cycle at least once per timeout even with zero traffic — so the signal is "did a poll cycle complete", never "did a message arrive". `telegram-front-desk-bot.ts`'s `pollLoop` now writes a heartbeat (`.swarmforge/operator/front-desk-poll-heartbeat.json`) on every completed cycle, success or handled failure alike, via `applyPollCycleResult`'s new `recordHeartbeat` hook — always the first thing that function does, before any other branch. `isPollCycleStale` (`telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts`) is the pure staleness decision (a `nil`/missing heartbeat also reads as stale, so a bot that never completed its first cycle is caught too); `front_desk_supervisor_lib.bb`'s `poll-heartbeat-stale?` mirrors the same predicate independently in Babashka (the same deliberate small-duplication-over-cross-language-coupling convention this codebase already uses elsewhere), so the actual restart decision never depends on the bot process's own event loop being alive to compute it. `front_desk_supervisor.bb`'s `check-one!` now treats a `"running"` entry with a stale heartbeat the same as a crash — a new `"stalled"` status feeding the same bounded-restart-with-backoff path the crash case already had (`FRONT_DESK_STALL_MS`, default 90s). Restarts stopping at the cap escalate loudly to the human by email, reusing `operator_lib.bb`'s existing BL-345 delivery-based alarm machinery (`classify-delivery-result` / `starvation-alarm-should-attempt?` / `next-starvation-alarm-state`) rather than a parallel implementation, so the escalation is armed only on confirmed delivery, never a bare send attempt — the alarm whose whole purpose is to break a silence must not itself fail silently.

**A human's park decision now survives host sleep and daemon restarts (BL-404).** Live incident, 2026-07-15: an operator/coordinator deliberately parked the front desk (killed the trio, wrote `.swarmforge/operator/front-desk-PARKED.md` — "DO NOT RESTART"); after the host slept and an unknown caller re-ran `start_handoff_daemon`, `launch_front_desk.sh` relaunched the trio and deleted the park flag, silently overriding the human's decision. The gap: `launch_front_desk.sh` decided whether to launch from its own supervisor pid-alive check and the `front-desk-supervisor.stop` file only — it never consulted the park flag, and unconditionally `rm -f`'d `.stop` on every launch regardless. The park flag now gets the same idempotent early-return posture the script already used for "already running", checked FIRST — before dry-run, token provisioning, and env validation — so a parked front desk never spawns anything or touches `.stop` regardless of caller or mode: if `front-desk-PARKED.md` exists, `launch_front_desk.sh` logs "front desk is PARKED; not launching" and exits 0. A park is now durable until a human explicitly lifts it. The other half of the gap the intake doc flagged: there was no lifting mechanism at all, only a manual `rm`. `unpark_front_desk.sh <project-root>` is the new explicit, auditable counterpart — removes the flag (or reports "not parked; nothing to do" if already absent) so a human has one clear command for the un-park side of the decision, not a bare filesystem edit.

**The front-desk supervisor now kills a superseded child before spawning its replacement, on every restart path (BL-403).** Live incident, 2026-07-15T07:39Z: supervisor pid 1881207 judged bot pid 1881442 (attempt-1) unhealthy, spawned replacement pid 1881899 (attempt-2), and left the first pid running — both long-polled Telegram `getUpdates` on the same bot token, which allows exactly one poller per token, so the second poller got HTTP 409 and the two bots fought over the offset. The existing code already guarded the forward direction ("a live pid is not proof it is still healthy"); this was the inverse gap — judging a child unhealthy never made its pid dead, and nothing confirmed that before spawning the replacement. `front_desk_supervisor.bb` gains `kill-pid!`: SIGTERM, a bounded grace period polling `ProcessHandle.isAlive`, then SIGKILL if still alive, so the prior pid is confirmed dead before `spawn!` ever runs. `check-one!` (`front_desk_supervisor_lib.bb`) takes `kill-pid!` as an optional 8th param (defaulting to a no-op so every pre-existing lower-arity caller/test is unaffected) and calls it on the entry's old `:pid`, guarded by `(:pid entry)`, in the `"waiting"`/`"stalled"` → restart branch. **Same-ticket follow-up:** the initial pass missed the `"gave-up"` → re-armed cooldown branch, which also spawns a fresh pid without ever having called `kill-pid!` — a gave-up entry's pid can still be genuinely alive, since `"gave-up"` is reachable from `"stalled"`, which BL-370 enters from `"running"` via `heartbeat-stale?` without ever checking `pid-alive?`. A hung-but-alive bot that exhausted its restart attempts and later re-armed could therefore get a second live pid alongside the never-killed first one, through the re-arm path this ticket exists to close. The re-arm branch now mirrors the restart branch exactly: `kill-pid!` on the old pid, guarded the same way, before `spawn!`. Exactly one bot process is now ever alive per supervisor, across both restart paths.

**The negotiation relay supervisor now kills a superseded relay child before spawning its replacement too (BL-411, the BL-403 gap on the OTHER caller).** BL-403 gave `front_desk_supervisor.bb`'s restart path a `kill-pid!` adapter so a judged-unhealthy child is confirmed dead before the replacement spawns, closing a live Telegram-409 incident on the front-desk bot. `negotiation_relay_supervisor.bb` is `check-one!`'s other caller — it reuses the same shared `front_desk_supervisor_lib.bb` state machine wholesale — but its `tick!` passed only 7 args, so `kill-pid!` silently defaulted to `check-one!`'s no-op and a restart never terminated the prior onboarding-negotiation poll-loop child: the identical two-pollers-on-one-bot-token exposure BL-403 closed for the front desk, left open on the relay. The termination semantics move to a shared constructor, `front-desk-supervisor-lib/make-kill-pid!` (SIGTERM, a bounded grace period polling `ProcessHandle.isAlive`, then SIGKILL), parameterised by grace-ms — `front_desk_supervisor.bb` and `negotiation_relay_supervisor.bb` each build their own `kill-pid!` from it (the relay's grace period configurable via `NEGOTIATION_RELAY_KILL_GRACE_MS`, default 2000ms) rather than the relay supervisor growing a second, hand-rolled copy that could drift from the first. `negotiation_relay_supervisor.bb`'s `tick!` now passes that adapter as `check-one!`'s 9th arg, so the "waiting"/"stalled" restart clause terminates and confirms-dead the prior relay pid before spawning the replacement, matching BL-403's coverage exactly (the "gave-up" re-arm and "not-started" spawn paths remain out of scope on both supervisors, unchanged from BL-403). Scope deliberately excludes `check-one!`'s own kill-before-spawn decision logic (that's BL-403's) and the relay's inner transient-retry/offset semantics (BL-369/389/394) — this ticket only wires the missing adapter through the second caller.

**The launcher no longer overwrites a role's own merged-but-unlanded script changes — the "phantom revert" root cause (BL-373).** `sync_worktree_scripts()` (`swarmforge/scripts/swarmforge.sh`) ran an unconditional `cp -R` of the launching checkout's `swarmforge/scripts/` and `swarmforge/profiles/` over every role worktree on every launch. In this self-hosted repo both paths are git-tracked, so the copy silently overwrote a role branch's own tracked content with the launching checkout's (usually `main`'s, often older) bytes, entirely outside git — six occurrences on 2026-07-14 alone, across four different roles, each one misdiagnosed as "hot-sync/tunnel drift" and hand-restored before the actual mechanism was found: a script change merged onto a role branch but not yet landed on `main` was silently reverted in that role's working tree at every relaunch. The sync itself is not gratuitous — a foreign target repo doesn't carry `swarmforge/` at all and genuinely needs the scripts copied in to be runnable — so the fix is not to delete it but to add the one check that separates the two cases: never overwrite a path the destination worktree's own git index tracks, since that content is git's to deliver (the role's branch already has it); where git doesn't track it, the copy remains the only delivery mechanism and keeps working exactly as before. `sync_worktree_scripts.bb`, backed by the pure `should-copy?` decision in `sync_worktree_scripts_lib.bb`, checks each file against the destination worktree's real `git ls-files` output before copying, and prints what it left to git rather than silently doing nothing — the previous silence is exactly how this defect survived six occurrences undiagnosed. The runtime-state copies into gitignored `.swarmforge/` (session/role tables, tmux socket/env files) are unaffected and still land on every launch.

**A ticket's pending approval is now asked in its own topic, not just left on a pull surface (BL-357).** The gap: a ticket's structured `human_approval` field fed only PULL surfaces (the PWA's needs-approval list, the briefing-email line) — nothing ever pushed it, so "please approve BL-338" was never actually asked; the human had to go looking. This is deliberately the *other* half of BL-295/BL-325's existing machinery: that loop already pushes a `NeedsApproval` notice into a ticket's topic when a role blocks *at a gate* while holding it — this ticket fires on the ticket's own `human_approval` field instead, a different signal through the same transport. `ApprovalRequested` is a fourth derived `SwarmEvent` (`swarmEventStream.ts`), firing only on the not-pending → pending *transition* of an ACTIVE ticket's `human_approval` (`EventStreamSnapshot.pendingApproval`, diffed the same false→true way `diffNeedsApproval` already diffs gates) — never a per-tick nag for a ticket that stays pending, and never for a paused ticket (most default to pending pre-promotion and have no topic yet). Routed through the existing topic machinery unchanged, reusing `messageTextForEvent` rather than a second formatter, with a plain-language ask ("Reply here with 'approve' to approve it."); a failed post is retried on the next tick via the same transition-held-back pattern `TaskStarted`/`NeedsApproval` already use. The genuinely new half is the write path back: `pendingApprovalReply.ts` recognizes an "approve" reply (a deliberate, auditable keyword match, not NLP — mirroring `backfill-human-approval.ts`'s own free-text classification posture) inside a ticket's own topic and flips that ticket's `human_approval` from pending to approved directly on its YAML, locating the file by its own `id:` field (never a filename guess, the same identity `backlogReader.ts` already treats as authoritative) and only ever touching a literal `human_approval: pending` line — never inventing the field on a ticket that lacks it, which stays `backfill-human-approval.ts`'s job. Wired into the live poll loop (`telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts`'s operator-context branch, `telegram-front-desk-bot.ts`'s `buildPollAdapters`) alongside the existing operator-context post, not instead of it. **Cleaner pass, same ticket:** the folder walk `pendingApprovalReply.ts` needed to find a ticket by id (active + paused, never done) was a byte-for-byte duplicate of `backfill-human-approval.ts`'s own readdir-with-missing-folder-tolerance loop; both now share one `forEachLiveTicketFile` (`extension/src/util/liveTicketFiles.ts`), with its own direct unit tests.

**A ticket's Telegram reply now supports `reject <reason>` and `amend <note>`, not just `approve` (BL-409).** The remaining two verbs from the original "approve/amend/reject an action" ask, building on BL-408's fix (accepting `pending-review` state and scanning paused tickets alongside active ones). `classifyApprovalReplyAction` (`pendingApprovalReply.ts`) is a three-way sibling of the existing `isApprovalReplyText` boolean (left unchanged, never overloaded): it checks anchored verb prefixes (`reject `, `amend `) before falling back to the older, unanchored `approve` match, so a reject/amend reply whose own reason/note happens to mention "approve" ("reject — needs a second approve from ops") still classifies by its own leading verb rather than the word buried inside it. A `reject <reason>` reply writes `human_approval: rejected  # <reason>` onto the ticket's backlog file (`rejectHumanApprovalText`/`recordRejectionReply`, the same targeted-line-replace shape as the existing approve path) and, because that flips `human_approval` off `pending`, the ticket's `ApprovalRequested` event stops re-firing on its own — no separate "stop asking" logic needed, it falls out of BL-357's existing not-pending → pending transition check. An `amend <note>` reply does **not** touch `human_approval` — the ticket stays pending — it only posts the extracted note (verb prefix stripped) as operator context via the existing `postOperatorContext` channel, so the specifier sees the amendment the next time it reviews the ticket. `deliverOperatorContext` (`telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts`) dispatches on the three-way classification: approve/reject/none keep posting the raw reply text as context (unchanged), amend posts only its stripped note, and reject additionally calls `recordRejectionReply`. Inline-keyboard buttons (a friendlier UI for the same three verbs) are explicitly out of scope here, tracked separately as BL-410.

**An `ApprovalRequested` Telegram message now carries Approve/Amend/Reject inline-keyboard buttons, a one-tap alternative to typing the reply (BL-410).** UX polish on top of BL-408/BL-409: `topicRouter.ts` attaches a `reply_markup.inline_keyboard` to the message it already posts on the not-pending → pending transition, and the front-desk bot's poll cycle (`telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts`) now recognizes the resulting `callback_query` update alongside its existing message-update handling — a `processUpdate` wrapper dispatches to either path, keeping the original message-update body (and its CRAP budget) untouched. Tapping Approve routes straight to the existing `recordApprovalReply` effect, the same write BL-357's typed "approve" reply triggers — no second approve implementation. Reject and Amend need a followup: Telegram delivers only a button id on tap, not free text, so tapping either stashes a pending reject/amend marker that the *next* bare topic reply resolves through BL-409's existing `recordRejectionReply` / operator-context effects; a tap with no followup text is a safe no-op, leaving the ticket pending exactly as before, rather than guessing at a reason/note that was never given. Every recognized callback_query gets an `answerCallbackQuery` response — including the no-op/stale-data cases — so the tap's loading spinner on the human's phone always clears. Typed replies (BL-408/BL-409) keep working unchanged; the button is a second entry point into the same verbs, not a new code path. **Cleaner pass, same ticket:** the new callback-query branch pushed `processUpdate` and a new `decideCallbackQueryAction` over the CRAP<=6 threshold; both were split the same way this file's own pre-existing `isFromMyChat`/`isFromPrincipal` pattern already does (extracting `isCallbackFromMyChat`/`isCallbackFromPrincipal`), a pure, behavior-preserving extraction confirmed by the full suite staying green.

**A ticket-topic reply no longer falsely claims "Nothing to approve right now" for a successful approve or for a genuine question on a still-pending ticket (BL-416).** Two live occurrences of the same wrong string, from two different causes: a successful "approve" reply got the generic fallback instead of a named confirmation, and — separately — a real, unrelated question asked inside a topic whose ticket was genuinely still pending got the identical "nothing to approve" text, which is factually false there (there IS something to approve: that exact ticket). Root cause was neither a race nor a keyword gap (BL-409 already covers the three verbs) but the fallback itself: `operator-decide.ts`'s reply path only ever recognised a role *currently gated on a live tmux prompt*, and fell through to one static, ticket-blind string for everything else. `composeTicketApprovalOverride` (`operator-decide.ts`) is a new, narrower check that only engages when the existing role-gate selector found nothing to do for this reply, using two new per-topic reads: `isTicketPendingApproval` (`pendingApprovalReply.ts`, a read-only counterpart to the existing approve/reject writers, scoped to this backlog id's own ticket file — never a global scan) and the existing `classifyApprovalReplyAction`'s verb classification. A successful approve now confirms `"<BL-id> approved."` by name instead of the generic text; a non-keyword reply on a still-pending ticket now answers `"<BL-id> is still awaiting approval - reply \"approve\" to confirm."` instead of denying anything is pending; a topic whose ticket is genuinely not pending is unaffected and still falls through to the original generic response. Scoping is per-ticket throughout — a pending ticket A and a resolved ticket B never share state, so a reply in one is judged only on that ticket's own `human_approval` field.

**An epic is now data, not prose — it gets its own topic and states the slice nobody ticketed yet (BL-341).** The gap: a multi-slice body of work (e.g. "Dynamic Routing") was invisible as a whole — Telegram topics are strictly per-ticket (`backlog-topic-map.json`), no `epic:` field existed anywhere in the schema, and an epic lived only as prose inside a ticket's `notes:` ("slice 2 of the dynamic-routing epic"). The human had hand-created three stopgap topics (recorded only in a local, unwired `epic-topic-map.json`) that this ticket had to adopt or replace — replace, per `swarmforge/backlog-schema.md`'s new `epic`/`type`/`remaining_slices` fields above. A slice now *declares* its epic (`epic: <id>`) as a real backlog field (`backlogReader.ts`), reusing an already-live convention this ticket found in actual use (BL-384's `type: epic`) rather than inventing a second one: the one item per epic id carrying `type: epic` *is* that epic's own definition — its title plus a human/specifier-authored `remaining_slices` list of work known to belong to the epic but not yet ticketed. `decideEpicTopicAction` (`topicRouter.ts`) looks up an epic's topic through the *same* `BacklogTopicMap` a ticket's own topic uses, keyed by epic id instead of `BL-###` — no second parallel map — creating a `"EPIC — <title>"` topic on the epic's first slice and reusing it after. Epic posts ride the *same* `TaskStarted`/`TaskCompleted` transitions that already drive per-ticket topic routing (`conciergeTick.ts`'s `postEpicUpdateIfApplicable`) — no new trigger: a slice's first appearance posts the epic's opening line, and a slice completing posts progress (`epicProgress.ts`) stating how many ticketed slices are done. The load-bearing requirement, and the easiest part to get wrong: `epicProgressText` never claims the epic complete while `remaining_slices` still lists untracked work, even when every *ticketed* slice is done — otherwise all three of the human's real epics would have read "done" while hiding the exact gap (BL-340, BL-343, BL-344 were ticketed precisely because this exercise surfaced them) he needed the view to expose. A ticket declaring no `epic:` is a no-op here, unchanged from today. **Same-commit fix found while touching this path:** `telegram-front-desk-bot.ts`'s `toFoldersSnapshot` silently dropped BL-357's `humanApproval` field (and would have dropped `epic`/`type`/`remainingSlices` the same way) on its way from the real backlog into the concierge tick — the pure logic was already correct and tested, but permanently dark against live data since unit tests inject fixtures directly rather than going through this narrowing; all four fields now pass through, regression-covered against real ticket fixtures.

**A deliberately-dropped Telegram message no longer parks the offset forever (BL-389).** Live incident, 2026-07-14: `backlog/topics/BL-359.json` was rewritten and pushed to `origin/main` roughly every 15 seconds for 44 minutes — 209 commits, the same two human messages replayed and re-answered on every poll, stopped only by a human taking the bot down. Root cause was a conflation, not the partial-failure hole the original incident report guessed: `processUpdate` (`telegramFrontDeskBotCore.ts`) returns `false` both for a delivery that genuinely *failed* and for a message *deliberately dropped* (not the principal, or no text) — and `pollAndForward` fed that single boolean into `offsetAfterDelivery` (BL-369's anti-message-loss guard), which parks the offset at the first `false` regardless of which kind it was. BL-369's guard is correct about failures — a failure may succeed on retry, so refusing to advance past it is right — but a drop is a *terminal* decision that can never succeed on retry, so refusing to advance past it is an unbounded retry of something that cannot complete: Telegram redelivers the parked update and everything after it, forever. `PollResult` already counted `posted` vs `dropped` separately; the fix threads that existing three-way distinction (delivered / dropped / failed) through to `offsetAfterDelivery` instead of collapsing it back to a boolean, so a drop now advances the offset and only a genuine failure still parks it. The redelivery this bug caused was harmless only where the side effect happened to be idempotent by `update_id` (`postToBridge`); `postOperatorContext`, `openSubjectAndRecord`, and `recordApprovalReply` were not, which is what let a single stuck update turn into 209 duplicate commits, so all three now dedupe on `update_id` too, closing the amplifier alongside the trigger. No human-visible command or setting changed; this is an internal front-desk reliability fix, verified against a fake update stream (dropped update advances past, failed one does not, an identical batch replayed twice records and answers exactly once) and, live, by restarting the front desk and confirming the parked backlog drained once with no new `BL topic record` commits beyond it.

**A no-op topic-record rewrite no longer mints a commit, so a redelivery loop upstream can't turn into unbounded git history (BL-390).** BL-329's `commitTopicRecord` committed on every call, including a rewrite that left the file byte-identical to what was already committed — harmless in isolation, but the push sweep (BL-356) pushes whatever lands on `main`, so a caller stuck re-persisting unchanged state (BL-389's dropped-message offset-park being one; a separate cost-health-sidecar churn of 6 commits in one minute on 2026-07-14 being another, same shape, different caller) turned into a stream of no-content commits reaching `origin/main`. `commitTopicRecord` (`blTopicStore.ts`) now checks `isFileCommitted` — already used elsewhere in this file to gate irreversible actions on durability — *before* attempting to commit at all, and returns early (already durable, nothing pushed) rather than falling through to `commitScopedFile`; a record that genuinely gained a message still commits exactly as before. The guard lives in the persister itself, not in any one caller, since this is the second time the same shape has hit two different callers in one day. **Hardener pass, same ticket:** `isFileCommitted`'s existing `git status --porcelain` check reads as empty output for a path with no pending changes — which is indistinguishable from a path that was simply never written at all; the function now checks the file exists first, so a never-written path always reads as "not committed" rather than colliding with the fail-closed contract `isRecordCommitted` (BL-331) already relies on for irreversible-action gating. No current caller triggers the collision (every caller here writes the file before checking it), but it sat directly upstream of this ticket's own new check-before-write path.

**Epic-progress Telegram announcements are change-gated, so a stuck retry can no longer flood the topic (BL-394).** Live incident, 2026-07-14: an epic's "EPIC — ..." topic received the same unchanging "N of M ticketed slice(s) complete." message once per concierge tick, indefinitely — distinct from BL-389/BL-390 (both shipped, both correctly not reproducing) and the third mechanism in the same front-desk-spam family. Root cause: `postEpicUpdateIfApplicable` (`conciergeTick.ts`) is a best-effort side effect layered on an already-routed `TaskStarted`/`TaskCompleted` event (BL-341) and, unlike `routeEvent`, had no dedup memory of its own — so whenever its triggering ticket event re-derived (most commonly a held-back retry after that *ticket's own* per-ticket post failed), it reposted the identical epic text on every such retry, with the epic's own aggregate never having moved. `epicAnnouncementKey` (`epicProgress.ts`) is a new content-based key (epic id + the exact announced text); `postEpicUpdateIfApplicable` now checks it against the *same* durable `emittedKeys` set the ticket-level events use before posting, and records it only after a successful send, mirroring `routeEvent`'s "only record what genuinely posted" contract. Any real change in the announced text — progress advancing, or the one-time BL-341 opening line — is a new key that announces once; an unchanged announcement is deduped forever, independent of how many times its triggering event re-derives, and survives a front-desk restart since the key lives in the same persisted tick state. No human-visible command or setting changed. **Hardener pass, same ticket:** extracted `processConciergeEvent`/`detectReadyChatId` as behavior-preserving splits to bring `runConciergeTick`/`provisionTelegramChannel` CRAP back under threshold — pre-existing complexity this ticket's one-line change happened to surface at function granularity, not a behavior change.

**The human is no longer sent raw terminal chrome scraped from an agent's pane (BL-391).** The human's own words, reacting to BL-359's first `NeedsApproval` message: "Can you make these messages more readable?" — that message was `\e[38;5;246m❯ \e[39m \e[38;5;244m─── ⏵⏵ bypass permissions on...`, ANSI colour codes and prompt/box-drawing chrome scraped verbatim from a tmux pane capture and posted as if it were prose. The category error: a pane capture is a TERMINAL RENDERING, not a message, and nothing sanitised it at the boundary where that text became human-facing. `stripTerminalChrome` (`extension/src/panel/needsHumanDetection.ts`) strips ANSI/CSI/OSC escape sequences and bare C0 control bytes (matching the standard CSI grammar, OSC sequences, and simpler Fe escape forms) while leaving Unicode glyphs — box-drawing characters, prompt arrows — untouched, since those are visible printable text, not control codes; ordinary prose, which never contains a control byte, passes through byte-identical. `extractQuestionSnippet` (the BL-073 needs-human email's question quote) now sanitises FIRST, before splitting into lines and before its length cap, so a truncated escape sequence can never leak a dangling partial code into the output and the character budget is spent on real content. Covers both destinations named in the ticket: the outbound message to the human and the durable topic record written to `backlog/topics/*.json` — the same unsanitised text would otherwise just move the noise into the git-committed copy.
